season">2018</a></li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><span style="font-weight:normal;"><b>West Division titles</b> (5)</span></th><td>\n<div class="hlist hlist-separated"><ul><li><a href="/wiki/1969_Atlanta_Braves_season" title="1969 Atlanta Braves season">1969</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/1982_Atlanta_Braves_season" title="1982 Atlanta Braves season">1982</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/1991_Atlanta_Braves_season" title="1991 Atlanta Braves season">1991</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/1992_Atlanta_Braves_season" title="1992 Atlanta Braves season">1992</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/1993_Atlanta_Braves_season" title="1993 Atlanta Braves season">1993</a></li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Wild card berths <span style="font-weight:normal;">(2)</span></th><td>\n<div class="hlist hlist-separated"><ul><li><a href="/wiki/2010_Atlanta_Braves_season" title="2010 Atlanta Braves season">2010</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/2012_Atlanta_Braves_season" title="2012 Atlanta Braves season">2012</a></li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color: #dcdcdc">Front office</th></tr><tr><th scope="row">Owner(s)</th><td>\n<a href="/wiki/Liberty_Media" title="Liberty Media">Liberty Media</a><sup id="cite_ref-BravesSaleApproved_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BravesSaleApproved-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Manager</th><td>\n<a href="/wiki/Brian_Snitker" title="Brian Snitker">Brian Snitker</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">General Manager</th><td>\n<a href="/wiki/Alex_Anthopoulos" title="Alex Anthopoulos">Alex Anthopoulos</a><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>Atlanta Braves</b> are an American <a href="/wiki/Professional_baseball" title="Professional baseball">professional baseball</a> franchise based in the <a href="/wiki/Atlanta_metropolitan_area" title="Atlanta metropolitan area">Atlanta metropolitan area</a>. The franchise competes in <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball" title="Major League Baseball">Major League Baseball</a> (MLB) as a member of the <a href="/wiki/National_League" title="National League">National League</a> (NL) <a href="/wiki/National_League_East" title="National League East">East</a> division. The Braves played home games at <a href="/wiki/Atlanta%E2%80%93Fulton_County_Stadium" title="Atlanta\xe2\x80\x93Fulton County Stadium">Atlanta\xe2\x80\x93Fulton County Stadium</a> from 1966 to 1996, and <a href="/wiki/Turner_Field" title="Turner Field">Turner Field</a> from 1997 to 2016. Since 2017, their home stadium has been <a href="/wiki/SunTrust_Park" title="SunTrust Park">SunTrust Park</a>, a new stadium 10 miles (16 km) northwest of <a href="/wiki/Downtown_Atlanta" title="Downtown Atlanta">downtown Atlanta</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Cumberland,_Georgia" title="Cumberland, Georgia">Cumberland</a> neighborhood of <a href="/wiki/Cobb_County" class="mw-redirect" title="Cobb County">Cobb County</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The Braves play <a href="/wiki/Spring_training" title="Spring training">spring training</a> games in <a href="/wiki/Lake_Buena_Vista,_Florida" title="Lake Buena Vista, Florida">Lake Buena Vista, Florida</a>. The Braves have agreed to move their <a href="/wiki/New_Braves_Spring_Training_Stadium" title="New Braves Spring Training Stadium">spring training home</a> to <a href="/wiki/North_Port,_Florida" title="North Port, Florida">North Port, Florida</a> in 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SHT2_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SHT2-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>The "Braves" name, which was first used in 1912, originates from a term for a Native American warrior. They are <a href="/wiki/List_of_baseball_nicknames" title="List of baseball nicknames">nicknamed</a> "the <i>Bravos</i>", and often referred to as "<a href="/wiki/America%27s_Team#Other_uses" title="America&#39;s Team">America\'s Team</a>" in reference to the team\'s games being broadcast on the nationally available <a href="/wiki/Braves_TBS_Baseball" title="Braves TBS Baseball">TBS</a> from the 1970s until 2007, giving the team a nationwide fan base.\n</p><p>From 1991 to 2005, the Braves were one of the most successful teams in baseball, winning division titles an unprecedented 14 consecutive times<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> (omitting the <a href="/wiki/1994%E2%80%9395_Major_League_Baseball_strike" title="1994\xe2\x80\x9395 Major League Baseball strike">strike-shortened</a> 1994 season in which there were no official division champions), and producing one of the greatest pitching rotations in the history of baseball. The Braves won the NL West 1991\xe2\x80\x9393 and the NL East 1995\xe2\x80\x932005, and they returned to the playoffs as the <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_wild_card" title="Major League Baseball wild card">National League Wild Card</a> in 2010. The Braves advanced to the <a href="/wiki/World_Series" title="World Series">World Series</a> five times in the 1990s, winning the title in 1995. Since their debut in the National League in 1876, the franchise has won 17 divisional titles, 17 <a href="/wiki/National_League_Championship_Series" title="National League Championship Series">National League</a> <a href="/wiki/Pennant_(sports)" title="Pennant (sports)">pennants</a>, and three World Series championships\xe2\x80\x94in <a href="/wiki/1914_World_Series" title="1914 World Series">1914</a> as the Boston Braves, in <a href="/wiki/1957_World_Series" title="1957 World Series">1957</a> as the Milwaukee Braves, and in <a href="/wiki/1995_World_Series" title="1995 World Series">1995</a> in Atlanta. The Braves are the only Major League Baseball franchise to have won the World Series in three different home cities.\n</p><p>The Braves and the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Cubs" title="Chicago Cubs">Chicago Cubs</a> are the National League\'s two remaining charter franchises. The Braves were founded in <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston, Massachusetts</a>, in 1871, as the <b>Boston Red Stockings</b> (not to be confused with the American League\'s <a href="/wiki/Boston_Red_Sox" title="Boston Red Sox">Boston Red Sox</a>). The team states it is "the oldest continuously operating <a href="/wiki/Major_professional_sports_teams_of_the_United_States_and_Canada" title="Major professional sports teams of the United States and Canada">professional sports franchise in America</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-BravesHistory_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BravesHistory-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After various name changes, the team eventually began operating as the <b>Boston Braves</b>, which lasted for most of the first half of the 20th century. Then, in 1953, the team <a href="/wiki/Relocation_of_professional_sports_teams" title="Relocation of professional sports teams">moved</a> to <a href="/wiki/Milwaukee" title="Milwaukee">Milwaukee, Wisconsin</a>, and became the <b>Milwaukee Braves</b>, followed by the final move to Atlanta in 1966. The team\'s tenure in Atlanta is noted for <a href="/wiki/Hank_Aaron" title="Hank Aaron">Hank Aaron</a> breaking <a href="/wiki/Babe_Ruth" title="Babe Ruth">Babe Ruth</a>\'s career home run record in 1974.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlanta_Braves&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Atlanta_Braves" title="History of the Atlanta Braves">History of the Atlanta Braves</a></div>\n<h3><span id="Boston_.281871.E2.80.931952.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Boston_(1871\xe2\x80\x931952)">Boston (1871\xe2\x80\x931952)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlanta_Braves&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Boston (1871\xe2\x80\x931952)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Boston_Braves" title="History of the Boston Braves">History of the Boston Braves</a></div>\n<h4><span id="1871.E2.80.931913"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1871\xe2\x80\x931913">1871\xe2\x80\x931913</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlanta_Braves&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1871\xe2\x80\x931913">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1873Bostonteampicture.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/1873Bostonteampicture.jpg/180px-1873Bostonteampicture.jpg" width="180" height="281" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/1873Bostonteampicture.jpg/270px-1873Bostonteampicture.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8e/1873Bostonteampicture.jpg 2x" data-file-width="298" data-file-height="466" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1873Bostonteampicture.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1873 Boston Red Stockings team picture; it finished first with a record of 43\xe2\x80\x9316</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Cincinnati_Red_Stockings" title="Cincinnati Red Stockings">Cincinnati Red Stockings</a>, established in 1869 as the first openly all-professional baseball team, voted to dissolve after the 1870 season. Player-manager <a href="/wiki/Harry_Wright" title="Harry Wright">Harry Wright</a>, with brother <a href="/wiki/George_Wright_(sportsman)" title="George Wright (sportsman)">George</a> and two other Cincinnati players, then went to Boston, Massachusetts at the invitation of Boston Red Stockings founder <a href="/wiki/Ivers_Whitney_Adams" title="Ivers Whitney Adams">Ivers Whitney Adams</a> to form the nucleus of the <i>Boston Red Stockings</i>, a charter member of the <a href="/wiki/National_Association_of_Professional_Base_Ball_Players" title="National Association of Professional Base Ball Players">National Association of Professional Base Ball Players</a> (NAPBBP). The original Boston Red Stockings team and its successors can lay claim to being the oldest continuously playing team in American professional sports.<sup id="cite_ref-BravesHistory_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BravesHistory-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> (The only other team that has been organized as long, the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Cubs" title="Chicago Cubs">Chicago Cubs</a>, did not play for the two years following the <a href="/wiki/Great_Chicago_Fire" title="Great Chicago Fire">Great Chicago Fire</a> of 1871.) Two young players hired away from the <a href="/wiki/Rockford_Forest_Citys" title="Rockford Forest Citys">Forest City club</a> of <a href="/wiki/Rockford,_Illinois" title="Rockford, Illinois">Rockford, Illinois</a>, turned out to be the biggest stars during the NAPBBP years: pitcher <a href="/wiki/Albert_Spalding" title="Albert Spalding">Al Spalding</a> (founder of <a href="/wiki/Spalding_(sports_equipment)" class="mw-redirect" title="Spalding (sports equipment)">Spalding</a> sporting goods) and second baseman <a href="/wiki/Ross_Barnes" title="Ross Barnes">Ross Barnes</a>.\n</p><p>Led by the Wright brothers, Barnes, and Spalding, the Red Stockings dominated the National Association, winning four of that league\'s five championships. The team became one of the National League\'s charter franchises in 1876, sometimes called the "<i>Red Caps</i>" (as a new Cincinnati Red Stockings club was another charter member). \n</p><p>The Boston Red Caps played in the first game in the history of <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball#Founding" title="Major League Baseball">the National League</a>, on Saturday, April 22, <a href="/wiki/1876_in_baseball" title="1876 in baseball">1876</a>, defeating the <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Athletics_(1860%E2%80%9376)" title="Philadelphia Athletics (1860\xe2\x80\x9376)">Athletics</a>, 6\xe2\x80\x935.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1888_Boston_Beaneaters.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/1888_Boston_Beaneaters.jpg/180px-1888_Boston_Beaneaters.jpg" width="180" height="121" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/1888_Boston_Beaneaters.jpg/270px-1888_Boston_Beaneaters.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/1888_Boston_Beaneaters.jpg/360px-1888_Boston_Beaneaters.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1426" data-file-height="959" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1888_Boston_Beaneaters.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1888 Boston Beaneaters</div></div></div>\n<p>Although somewhat stripped of talent in the National League\'s inaugural year, Boston bounced back to win the 1877 and 1878 pennants. The Red Caps/Beaneaters were one of the league\'s dominant teams during the 19th century, winning a total of eight pennants. For most of that time, their <a href="/wiki/Manager_(baseball)" title="Manager (baseball)">manager</a> was <a href="/wiki/Frank_Selee" title="Frank Selee">Frank Selee</a>. Boston came to be called the <i>Beaneaters</i> in 1883, while retaining red as the team color. The <a href="/wiki/1898_Boston_Beaneaters_season" title="1898 Boston Beaneaters season">1898 team</a> finished 102\xe2\x80\x9347, a club record for wins that would stand for <a href="/wiki/1993_Atlanta_Braves_season" title="1993 Atlanta Braves season">almost a century</a>. Stars of those 1890s Beaneater teams included the "Heavenly Twins", <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Duffy" title="Hugh Duffy">Hugh Duffy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tommy_McCarthy" title="Tommy McCarthy">Tommy McCarthy</a>, as well as "Slidin\'" <a href="/wiki/Billy_Hamilton_(baseball,_born_1866)" title="Billy Hamilton (baseball, born 1866)">Billy Hamilton</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hugh_Duffy_Baseball.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/17/Hugh_Duffy_Baseball.jpg/180px-Hugh_Duffy_Baseball.jpg" width="180" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/17/Hugh_Duffy_Baseball.jpg/270px-Hugh_Duffy_Baseball.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/17/Hugh_Duffy_Baseball.jpg/360px-Hugh_Duffy_Baseball.jpg 2x" data-file-width="375" data-file-height="333" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hugh_Duffy_Baseball.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Hugh_Duffy" title="Hugh Duffy">Hugh Duffy</a> played with the franchise from 1892 to 1900 and won the third <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_Triple_Crown" class="mw-redirect" title="Major League Baseball Triple Crown">Triple Crown</a> in MLB history</div></div></div>\n<p>The team was decimated when the <a href="/wiki/American_League" title="American League">American League</a>\'s new Boston entry set up shop in 1901. Many of the Beaneaters\' stars jumped to the new team, which offered contracts that the Beaneaters\' owners did not even bother to match. They only managed one winning season from 1900 to 1913, and lost 100 games five times. In 1907, the Beaneaters (temporarily) eliminated the last bit of red from their stockings because their manager thought the red dye could cause wounds to become infected (as noted in <i>The Sporting News Baseball Guide</i> during the 1940s when each team\'s entry had a <a href="/wiki/History_of_baseball_team_nicknames" title="History of baseball team nicknames">history of its nickname(s)</a>. The American League club\'s owner, Charles Taylor, wasted little time in adopting <a href="/wiki/Boston_Red_Sox" title="Boston Red Sox">Red Sox</a> as his team\'s first official nickname (up to that point they had been called by the generic "Americans"). Media-driven nickname changes to the <i>Doves</i> in 1907 and the <i>Rustlers</i> in 1911 did nothing to change the National League club\'s luck. The team became the <i>Braves</i> for the first time in 1912. Their owner, <a href="/wiki/James_E._Gaffney" title="James E. Gaffney">James Gaffney</a>, was a member of New York City\'s political machine, <a href="/wiki/Tammany_Hall" title="Tammany Hall">Tammany Hall</a>, which used an Indian chief as their symbol.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="1914:_Miracle">1914: Miracle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlanta_Braves&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1914: Miracle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1914_Boston_Braves_season" title="1914 Boston Braves season">1914 Boston Braves season</a></div>\n<p>Two years later, the Braves put together one of the most memorable seasons in baseball history. After a dismal 4\xe2\x80\x9318 start, the Braves seemed to be on pace for a last place finish. On July 4, 1914, the Braves lost both games of a doubleheader to the <a href="/wiki/1914_Brooklyn_Dodgers_season" class="mw-redirect" title="1914 Brooklyn Dodgers season">Brooklyn Dodgers</a>. The consecutive losses put their record at 26\xe2\x80\x9340 and the Braves were in last place, <i>15 games</i> behind the league-leading <a href="/wiki/1914_New_York_Giants_season" title="1914 New York Giants season">New York Giants</a>, who had won the previous three league pennants. After a day off, the Braves started to put together a hot streak, and from July 6 through September 5, the Braves went 41\xe2\x80\x9312.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> On September 7 and 8, the Braves took two of three games from the New York Giants and moved into first place. The Braves tore through September and early October, closing with 25 wins against six losses, while the Giants went 16\xe2\x80\x9316.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> They were the only team, under the old eight-team league format, to win a pennant after being in last place on the Fourth of July. They were in last place as late as July 18, but were close to the pack, moving into fourth on July 21 and second place on August 12.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Seattle_Bill_James.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Seattle_Bill_James.jpg/220px-Seattle_Bill_James.jpg" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Seattle_Bill_James.jpg/330px-Seattle_Bill_James.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Seattle_Bill_James.jpg/440px-Seattle_Bill_James.jpg 2x" data-file-width="920" data-file-height="656" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Seattle_Bill_James.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Starting pitcher <a href="/wiki/Bill_James_(pitcher,_born_1892)" title="Bill James (pitcher, born 1892)">Bill James</a> was 2\xe2\x80\x930 in the <a href="/wiki/1914_World_Series" title="1914 World Series">1914 World Series</a>, and the Braves recorded the first sweep in World Series history</div></div></div>\n<p>Despite their amazing comeback, the Braves entered the <a href="/wiki/1914_World_Series" title="1914 World Series">World Series</a> as a heavy underdog to <a href="/wiki/Connie_Mack" title="Connie Mack">Connie Mack</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/1914_Philadelphia_Athletics_season" title="1914 Philadelphia Athletics season">Philadelphia A\'s</a>. Nevertheless, the Braves swept the Athletics\xe2\x80\x94the first unqualified sweep in the young history of the modern <a href="/wiki/World_Series" title="World Series">World Series</a> (the <a href="/wiki/1907_World_Series" title="1907 World Series">1907 Series</a> had one tied game) to win the world championship. Meanwhile, <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Evers" title="Johnny Evers">Johnny Evers</a> won the <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_Most_Valuable_Player_Award" title="Major League Baseball Most Valuable Player Award">Chalmers Award</a>.\n</p><p>The Braves played the World Series (as well as the last few games of the 1914 season) at <a href="/wiki/Fenway_Park" title="Fenway Park">Fenway Park</a>, since their normal home, the <a href="/wiki/South_End_Grounds" title="South End Grounds">South End Grounds</a>, was too small. However, the Braves\' success inspired owner Gaffney to build a modern park, <a href="/wiki/Braves_Field" title="Braves Field">Braves Field</a>, which opened in August 1915. It was the largest park in the majors at the time, with 40,000 seats and a very spacious outfield. The park was novel for its time; public transportation brought fans right to the park.\n</p>\n<h4><span id="1915.E2.80.931953"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1915\xe2\x80\x931953">1915\xe2\x80\x931953</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlanta_Braves&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1915\xe2\x80\x931953">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>After contending for most of 1915 and 1916, the Braves only twice posted winning records from 1917 to 1932. The lone highlight of those years came when Judge <a href="/wiki/Emil_Fuchs_(baseball)" title="Emil Fuchs (baseball)">Emil Fuchs</a> bought the team in 1923 to bring his longtime friend, pitching great <a href="/wiki/Christy_Mathewson" title="Christy Mathewson">Christy Mathewson</a>, back into the game. However, Mathewson died in 1925, leaving Fuchs in control of the team.\n</p><p>Fuchs was committed to building a winner, but the damage from the years prior to his arrival took some time to overcome. The Braves finally managed to be competitive in 1933 and 1934 under manager <a href="/wiki/Bill_McKechnie" title="Bill McKechnie">Bill McKechnie</a>, but Fuchs\' revenue was severely depleted due to the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>.\n</p><p>Looking for a way to get more fans and more money, Fuchs worked out a deal with the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Yankees" title="New York Yankees">New York Yankees</a> to acquire <a href="/wiki/Babe_Ruth" title="Babe Ruth">Babe Ruth</a>, who had started his career with the Red Sox. Fuchs made Ruth team vice president, and promised him a share of the profits. He was also granted the title of assistant manager, and was to be consulted on all of the Braves\' deals. Fuchs even suggested that Ruth, who had long had his heart set on managing, could take over as manager once McKechnie stepped down\xe2\x80\x94perhaps as early as 1936.<sup id="cite_ref-Neyer_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Neyer-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At first, it appeared that Ruth was the final piece the team needed in 1935. On opening day, he had a hand in all of the Braves\' runs in a 4\xe2\x80\x932 win over the <a href="/wiki/1936_New_York_Giants_(MLB)_season" title="1936 New York Giants (MLB) season">Giants</a>. However, that proved to be the only time the Braves were over .500 all year. Events went downhill quickly. While Ruth could still hit, he could do little else. He could not run, and his fielding was so terrible that three of the Braves\' pitchers threatened to go on strike if Ruth were in the lineup. It soon became obvious that he was vice president and assistant manager in name only and Fuchs\' promise of a share of team profits was hot air. In fact, Ruth discovered that Fuchs expected him to invest some of <i>his</i> money in the team.<sup id="cite_ref-Neyer_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Neyer-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Seeing a franchise in complete disarray, Ruth retired on June 1\xe2\x80\x94only six days after he clouted what turned out to be the last three <a href="/wiki/Home_run" title="Home run">home runs</a> of his career. He had wanted to quit as early as May 12, but Fuchs wanted him to hang on so he could play in every National League park.<sup id="cite_ref-Neyer_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Neyer-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The Braves finished 38\xe2\x80\x93115, the worst season in franchise history. Their .248 winning percentage is <a href="/wiki/List_of_worst_MLB_season_records" class="mw-redirect" title="List of worst MLB season records">the third-worst in baseball history</a>, and the second-worst in National League history (behind only the <a href="/wiki/1899_Cleveland_Spiders_season" title="1899 Cleveland Spiders season">1899 Cleveland Spiders</a>).\n</p><p>Fuchs lost control of the team in August 1935,<sup id="cite_ref-Neyer_21-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Neyer-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> and the new owners tried to change the team\'s image by renaming it the <i>Boston Bees</i>. This did little to change the team\'s fortunes. After five uneven years, a new owner, construction magnate <a href="/wiki/Lou_Perini" title="Lou Perini">Lou Perini</a>, changed the nickname back to the Braves. He immediately set about rebuilding the team. World War II slowed things down a little, but the team rode the pitching of <a href="/wiki/Warren_Spahn" title="Warren Spahn">Warren Spahn</a> to impressive seasons in 1946 and 1947.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Johnny_Sain_and_Warren_Spahn.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Johnny_Sain_and_Warren_Spahn.png/220px-Johnny_Sain_and_Warren_Spahn.png" width="220" height="311" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e3/Johnny_Sain_and_Warren_Spahn.png 1.5x" data-file-width="295" data-file-height="417" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Johnny_Sain_and_Warren_Spahn.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Spahn (right) with <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Sain" title="Johnny Sain">Johnny Sain</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1948, the <a href="/wiki/1948_Boston_Braves_season" title="1948 Boston Braves season">team</a> won the pennant, behind the pitching of Spahn and <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Sain" title="Johnny Sain">Johnny Sain</a>, who won 39 games between them. The remainder of the rotation was so thin that in September, <i>Boston Post</i> writer Gerald Hern wrote this poem about the pair:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><i>First we\'ll use Spahn</i></dd>\n<dd><i>then we\'ll use Sain</i></dd>\n<dd><i>Then an off day</i></dd>\n<dd><i>followed by rain</i></dd>\n<dd><i>Back will come Spahn</i></dd>\n<dd><i>followed by Sain</i></dd>\n<dd><i>And followed</i></dd>\n<dd><i>we hope</i></dd>\n<dd><i>by two days of rain.</i></dd></dl>\n<p>The poem received such a wide audience that the sentiment, usually now paraphrased as <i>"Spahn and Sain and pray for rain"</i>, entered the baseball vocabulary. However, in the 1948 season, the Braves actually had the same record in games that Spahn and Sain started that the team had overall, in terms of winning percentage.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/1948_World_Series" title="1948 World Series">1948 World Series</a>, which the Braves lost in six games to the <a href="/wiki/1948_Cleveland_Indians_season" title="1948 Cleveland Indians season">Indians</a>, turned out to be the Braves\' last hurrah in Boston. In 1950, <a href="/wiki/Sam_Jethroe" title="Sam Jethroe">Sam Jethroe</a> became the team\'s first African American player, making his major league debut on April 18. Amid four mediocre seasons, attendance steadily dwindled until, on March 13, 1953, Perini, who had recently bought out his original partners, announced he was moving the team to <a href="/wiki/Milwaukee" title="Milwaukee">Milwaukee</a>, where the Braves had their top farm club, the <a href="/wiki/Milwaukee_Brewers_minor_league" class="mw-redirect" title="Milwaukee Brewers minor league">Brewers</a>. Milwaukee had long been a possible target for relocation. <a href="/wiki/Bill_Veeck" title="Bill Veeck">Bill Veeck</a> had tried to return his <a href="/wiki/Saint_Louis_Browns" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Louis Browns">St. Louis Browns</a> there earlier the same year (Milwaukee was the original home of that franchise), but his proposal had been voted down by the other American League owners.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Milwaukee_.281953.E2.80.931965.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Milwaukee_(1953\xe2\x80\x931965)">Milwaukee (1953\xe2\x80\x931965)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlanta_Braves&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Milwaukee (1953\xe2\x80\x931965)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Atlanta_Braves#Milwaukee" title="History of the Atlanta Braves">History of the Atlanta Braves \xc2\xa7&#160;Milwaukee</a></div>\n<p>Milwaukee went wild over the Braves, drawing a then-NL record 1.8&#160;million fans. The Braves finished 92\xe2\x80\x9362 in their first season in Milwaukee. The success of the relocated team showed that baseball could succeed in new markets, and the <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Athletics" class="mw-redirect" title="Philadelphia Athletics">Philadelphia Athletics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Louis_Browns" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Louis Browns">St. Louis Browns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn_Dodgers" class="mw-redirect" title="Brooklyn Dodgers">Brooklyn Dodgers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/New_York_Giants_(NL)" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Giants (NL)">New York Giants</a> would leave their hometowns within the next five years.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MilwaukeeBaseballM.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/MilwaukeeBaseballM.svg/140px-MilwaukeeBaseballM.svg.png" width="140" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/MilwaukeeBaseballM.svg/210px-MilwaukeeBaseballM.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/MilwaukeeBaseballM.svg/280px-MilwaukeeBaseballM.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="245" data-file-height="246" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MilwaukeeBaseballM.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Milwaukee Braves cap logo</div></div></div>\n<p>As the 1950s progressed, the reinvigorated Braves became increasingly competitive. Sluggers <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Mathews" title="Eddie Mathews">Eddie Mathews</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hank_Aaron" title="Hank Aaron">Hank Aaron</a> drove the offense (they would hit a combined 1,226 home runs as Braves, with 850 of those coming while the franchise was in Milwaukee), often aided by another power hitter, <a href="/wiki/Joe_Adcock" title="Joe Adcock">Joe Adcock</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Warren_Spahn" title="Warren Spahn">Warren Spahn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lew_Burdette" title="Lew Burdette">Lew Burdette</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bob_Buhl" title="Bob Buhl">Bob Buhl</a> anchored the rotation. The 1956 Braves finished second, only one game behind the Brooklyn Dodgers.\n</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/1957_Milwaukee_Braves_season" title="1957 Milwaukee Braves season">1957</a>, the Braves celebrated their first pennant in nine years spearheaded by Aaron\'s <a href="/wiki/MLB_Most_Valuable_Player_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="MLB Most Valuable Player Award">MVP season</a>, as he led the National League in home runs and RBI. Perhaps the most memorable of his 44 round-trippers that season came on September 23, a two-run <a href="/wiki/Walk-off_home_run" title="Walk-off home run">walk-off home run</a> that gave the Braves a 4\xe2\x80\x932 victory over the <a href="/wiki/1957_St._Louis_Cardinals_season" title="1957 St. Louis Cardinals season">St. Louis Cardinals</a> and clinched the League championship. The team then went on to its first <a href="/wiki/World_Series" title="World Series">World Series</a> win in over 40 years, defeating the powerful <a href="/wiki/1957_New_York_Yankees_season" title="1957 New York Yankees season">New York Yankees</a> of <a href="/wiki/Yogi_Berra" title="Yogi Berra">Berra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mickey_Mantle" title="Mickey Mantle">Mantle</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Whitey_Ford" title="Whitey Ford">Ford</a> in seven games. One-time Yankee Burdette, the Series MVP, threw three complete game victories against his former team, giving up only two earned runs.\n</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/1958_Milwaukee_Braves_season" title="1958 Milwaukee Braves season">1958</a>, the Braves again won the National League pennant and jumped out to a three games to one lead in the World Series against the <a href="/wiki/1958_New_York_Yankees_season" title="1958 New York Yankees season">New York Yankees</a> once more, thanks in part to the strength of Spahn\'s and Burdette\'s pitching. But the Yankees stormed back to take the last three games, in large part to World Series MVP <a href="/wiki/Bob_Turley" title="Bob Turley">Bob Turley</a>\'s pitching.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/1959_Milwaukee_Braves_season" title="1959 Milwaukee Braves season">1959 season</a> saw the Braves finish the season in a tie with the <a href="/wiki/1959_Los_Angeles_Dodgers_season" title="1959 Los Angeles Dodgers season">Los Angeles Dodgers</a>, both with 86\xe2\x80\x9368 records. Many residents of Chicago and Milwaukee were hoping for a Sox-Braves Series, as the cities are only about 75 miles (121&#160;km) apart, but it was not to be because Milwaukee fell in a best-of-3 playoff with two straight losses to the Dodgers. The Dodgers would go on to defeat the <a href="/wiki/1959_Chicago_White_Sox_season" title="1959 Chicago White Sox season">Chicago White Sox</a> in the <a href="/wiki/1959_World_Series" title="1959 World Series">World Series</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MilwaukeeBraves5461.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/b/b9/MilwaukeeBraves5461.png" width="120" height="120" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="120" data-file-height="120" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MilwaukeeBraves5461.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Milwaukee Braves logo (1953\xe2\x80\x931956)</div></div></div>\n<p>The next six years were up-and-down for the Braves. The 1960 season featured two no-hitters by Burdette and Spahn, and Milwaukee finished seven games behind the <a href="/wiki/1960_Pittsburgh_Pirates_season" title="1960 Pittsburgh Pirates season">Pittsburgh Pirates</a>, who ultimately were to win the <a href="/wiki/1960_World_Series" title="1960 World Series">World Series</a> that year, in second place, one year after the Braves were on the winning end of the 13-inning near-perfect game of Pirates pitcher <a href="/wiki/Harvey_Haddix" title="Harvey Haddix">Harvey Haddix</a>. The 1961 season saw a drop in the standings for the Braves down to fourth, despite Spahn recording his 300th victory and pitching another no-hitter that year.\n</p><p>Aaron hit 45 <a href="/wiki/Home_run" title="Home run">home runs</a> in 1962, a Milwaukee career high for him, but this did not translate into wins for the Braves, as they finished fifth. <a href="/wiki/1963_in_baseball" title="1963 in baseball">The next season</a>, Aaron again hit 44 home runs and notched 130 RBI, and Spahn was once again the ace of the staff, going 23\xe2\x80\x937. However, none of the other Braves produced at that level, and the team finished in the "<a href="/wiki/First_division_(baseball)" title="First division (baseball)">second division</a>", for the first time in its short history in Milwaukee.\n</p><p>The Braves were mediocre as the 1960s began, with an inflated win total fed by the expansion <a href="/wiki/New_York_Mets" title="New York Mets">New York Mets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Houston_Colt_.45s" class="mw-redirect" title="Houston Colt .45s">Houston Colt .45s</a>. To this day, the Milwaukee Braves are the only major league team who played more than one season and never had a losing record.\n</p><p>Perini sold the Braves to a Chicago-based group led by <a href="/wiki/William_Bartholomay" title="William Bartholomay">William Bartholomay</a> in 1962. Almost immediately Bartholomay started shopping the Braves to a larger television market. Keen to attract them, the fast-growing city of Atlanta, led by Mayor\n<a href="/wiki/Ivan_Allen,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Ivan Allen, Jr.">Ivan Allen, Jr.</a> constructed a new $18&#160;million, 52,000-seat ballpark in less than one year, <a href="/wiki/Atlanta_Stadium" class="mw-redirect" title="Atlanta Stadium">Atlanta Stadium</a>, which was officially opened in 1965 in hopes of luring an existing major league baseball and/or NFL/AFL team. After the city failed to lure the <a href="/wiki/Kansas_City_Athletics" class="mw-redirect" title="Kansas City Athletics">Kansas City A\'s</a> to Atlanta (the A\'s would move to Oakland in 1968), the Braves announced their intention to move to Atlanta for the 1965 season. However, an injunction filed in Wisconsin kept the Braves in Milwaukee for one final year. In 1966, the Braves completed the move to Atlanta.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Eddie_Mathews" title="Eddie Mathews">Eddie Mathews</a> is the only Braves player to have played for the organization in all three cities that they have been based in. Mathews played with the Braves for their last season in Boston, the team\'s entire tenure in Milwaukee, and their first season in Atlanta.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Atlanta_.281966.E2.80.93present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Atlanta_(1966\xe2\x80\x93present)">Atlanta (1966\xe2\x80\x93present)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlanta_Braves&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Atlanta (1966\xe2\x80\x93present)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span id="1966.E2.80.931974"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1966\xe2\x80\x931974">1966\xe2\x80\x931974</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlanta_Braves&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 1966\xe2\x80\x931974">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The Braves were a .500 team in their first few years in Atlanta; 85\xe2\x80\x9377 in 1966, 77\xe2\x80\x9385 in 1967, and 81\xe2\x80\x9381 in 1968. The 1967 season was the Braves\' first losing season since 1952, their last year in Boston. In 1969, with the onset of divisional play, the Braves won the first-ever <a href="/wiki/National_League_West" title="National League West">National League West</a> Division title, before being swept by the "<a href="/wiki/1969_New_York_Mets_season" title="1969 New York Mets season">Miracle Mets</a>" in the <a href="/wiki/1969_National_League_Championship_Series" title="1969 National League Championship Series">National League Championship Series</a>. They would not be a factor during the next decade, posting only two winning seasons between 1970 and 1981 \xe2\x80\x93 in some cases, fielding teams as bad as the worst Boston teams.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aaron_715.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Aaron_715.jpg/170px-Aaron_715.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Aaron_715.jpg/255px-Aaron_715.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Aaron_715.jpg/340px-Aaron_715.jpg 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="640" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aaron_715.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The fence over which Hank Aaron hit his 715th career home run still exists outside of <a href="/wiki/Turner_Field" title="Turner Field">Turner Field</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>In the meantime, fans had to be satisfied with the achievements of <a href="/wiki/Hank_Aaron" title="Hank Aaron">Hank Aaron</a>. In the relatively hitter-friendly confines and higher-than-average altitude of Atlanta Stadium ("The Launching Pad"), he actually increased his offensive production. Atlanta also produced batting champions in <a href="/wiki/Rico_Carty" title="Rico Carty">Rico Carty</a> (in 1970) and <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Garr" title="Ralph Garr">Ralph Garr</a> (in 1974). In the shadow of Aaron\'s historical home run pursuit, was the fact that three Atlanta sluggers hit 40 or more home runs in 1973 \xe2\x80\x93 <a href="/wiki/Darrell_Evans" title="Darrell Evans">Darrell Evans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Davey_Johnson" title="Davey Johnson">Davey Johnson</a> along with Aaron.\n</p><p>By the end of the 1973 season, Aaron had hit 713 home runs, one short of Ruth\'s record. Throughout the winter he received racially motivated death threats, but stood up well under the pressure. The <a href="/wiki/1974_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1974 Major League Baseball season">next season</a>, it was only a matter of time before he set a new record. On April 4, opening day, he hit No.714 in <a href="/wiki/1974_Cincinnati_Reds_season" title="1974 Cincinnati Reds season">Cincinnati</a>, and on April 8, in front of his home fans and a national television audience he finally beat Ruth\'s mark with a home run to left-center field off left-hander <a href="/wiki/Al_Downing_(baseball)" title="Al Downing (baseball)">Al Downing</a> of the <a href="/wiki/1974_Los_Angeles_Dodgers_season" title="1974 Los Angeles Dodgers season">Los Angeles Dodgers</a>. Aaron spent most of his career as a Milwaukee and Atlanta Brave before asking to be traded to the <a href="/wiki/Milwaukee_Brewers" title="Milwaukee Brewers">Milwaukee Brewers</a>, while Ruth finished his career as a Boston Brave. In fact, until <a href="/wiki/Barry_Bonds" title="Barry Bonds">Barry Bonds</a> eclipsed the 714 home runs hit by <a href="/wiki/Babe_Ruth" title="Babe Ruth">Babe Ruth</a> in 2006, the top two home run hitters in Major League history had at one time been Braves.\n</p>\n<h4><span id="1976.E2.80.931977:_Ted_Turner_buys_the_team"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1976\xe2\x80\x931977:_Ted_Turner_buys_the_team">1976\xe2\x80\x931977: Ted Turner buys the team</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlanta_Braves&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 1976\xe2\x80\x931977: Ted Turner buys the team">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%D0%9C%D0%B5%D0%B6%D0%B4%D1%83%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%9B%D0%B5%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B4%D0%BE-%D0%BF%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%B8%D1%8F_10.1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/%D0%9C%D0%B5%D0%B6%D0%B4%D1%83%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%9B%D0%B5%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B4%D0%BE-%D0%BF%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%B8%D1%8F_10.1.jpg/220px-%D0%9C%D0%B5%D0%B6%D0%B4%D1%83%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%9B%D0%B5%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B4%D0%BE-%D0%BF%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%B8%D1%8F_10.1.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/%D0%9C%D0%B5%D0%B6%D0%B4%D1%83%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%9B%D0%B5%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B4%D0%BE-%D0%BF%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%B8%D1%8F_10.1.jpg/330px-%D0%9C%D0%B5%D0%B6%D0%B4%D1%83%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%9B%D0%B5%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B4%D0%BE-%D0%BF%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%B8%D1%8F_10.1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/%D0%9C%D0%B5%D0%B6%D0%B4%D1%83%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%9B%D0%B5%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B4%D0%BE-%D0%BF%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%B8%D1%8F_10.1.jpg/440px-%D0%9C%D0%B5%D0%B6%D0%B4%D1%83%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%9B%D0%B5%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B4%D0%BE-%D0%BF%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%B8%D1%8F_10.1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1152" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%D0%9C%D0%B5%D0%B6%D0%B4%D1%83%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%9B%D0%B5%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B4%D0%BE-%D0%BF%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%B8%D1%8F_10.1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Media magnate <a href="/wiki/Ted_Turner" title="Ted Turner">Ted Turner</a> purchased the team in 1976, and played a large role in the team\'s operation.</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1976, the team was purchased by media magnate <a href="/wiki/Ted_Turner" title="Ted Turner">Ted Turner</a>, owner of <a href="/wiki/Superstation" title="Superstation">superstation</a> <a href="/wiki/TBS_(TV_network)" class="mw-redirect" title="TBS (TV network)">WTBS</a>, as a means to keep the team (and one of his main programming staples) in Atlanta. The financially strapped Turner used money already paid to the team for their broadcast rights as a down-payment. It was then that Atlanta Stadium was renamed <a href="/wiki/Atlanta%E2%80%93Fulton_County_Stadium" title="Atlanta\xe2\x80\x93Fulton County Stadium">Atlanta\xe2\x80\x93Fulton County Stadium</a>. Turner quickly gained a reputation as a quirky, hands-on baseball owner. On May 11, 1977, Turner appointed himself <a href="/wiki/Manager_(baseball)" title="Manager (baseball)">manager</a>, but because MLB passed a rule in the 1950s barring managers from holding a financial stake in their teams, Turner was ordered to relinquish that position after one game (the <a href="/wiki/1977_Atlanta_Braves_season" title="1977 Atlanta Braves season">Braves</a> lost 2\xe2\x80\x931 to the <a href="/wiki/1977_Pittsburgh_Pirates_season" title="1977 Pittsburgh Pirates season">Pittsburgh Pirates</a> to bring their losing streak to 17 games).\n</p><p>Turner used the Braves as a major programming draw for his fledgling cable network, making the Braves the first franchise to have a nationwide audience and fan base. WTBS marketed the team as "The Atlanta Braves: <a href="/wiki/America%27s_Team" title="America&#39;s Team">America\'s Team</a>", a nickname that still sticks in some areas of the country, especially <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">the South</a>. Among other things, in 1976 Turner suggested the nickname "Channel" for pitcher <a href="/wiki/Andy_Messersmith" title="Andy Messersmith">Andy Messersmith</a> and jersey number 17, in order to promote the television station that aired <a href="/wiki/Braves_TBS_Baseball" title="Braves TBS Baseball">Braves games</a>. Major League Baseball quickly nixed the idea.\n</p>\n<h4><span id="1978.E2.80.931990"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1978\xe2\x80\x931990">1978\xe2\x80\x931990</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlanta_Braves&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: 1978\xe2\x80\x931990">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PhilNierko1982.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/PhilNierko1982.jpg/220px-PhilNierko1982.jpg" width="220" height="205" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/PhilNierko1982.jpg/330px-PhilNierko1982.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/PhilNierko1982.jpg/440px-PhilNierko1982.jpg 2x" data-file-width="782" data-file-height="727" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PhilNierko1982.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Phil_Niekro" title="Phil Niekro">Phil Niekro</a> played with the Braves from 1964 to 1983 and in 1987; he holds the MLB record for most wins by a <a href="/wiki/Knuckleball" title="Knuckleball">knuckleball</a> pitcher</div></div></div>\n<p>After three straight losing seasons, <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Cox" title="Bobby Cox">Bobby Cox</a> was hired for his first stint as manager for the 1978 season. He promoted 22-year-old slugger <a href="/wiki/Dale_Murphy" title="Dale Murphy">Dale Murphy</a> into the starting lineup. Murphy hit 77 home runs over the next three seasons, but he struggled on defense, unable to adeptly play either catcher or first base. In 1980, Murphy was moved to center field and demonstrated excellent range and throwing ability, while the Braves earned their first winning season since 1974. Cox was fired after the 1981 season and replaced with <a href="/wiki/Joe_Torre" title="Joe Torre">Joe Torre</a>, under whose leadership the Braves attained their first divisional title since 1969. Strong performances from <a href="/wiki/Bob_Horner" title="Bob Horner">Bob Horner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chris_Chambliss" title="Chris Chambliss">Chris Chambliss</a>, pitcher <a href="/wiki/Phil_Niekro" title="Phil Niekro">Phil Niekro</a>, and short relief pitcher <a href="/wiki/Gene_Garber" title="Gene Garber">Gene Garber</a> helped the Braves, but no Brave was more acclaimed than Murphy, who won both a <a href="/wiki/MLB_Most_Valuable_Player_award" class="mw-redirect" title="MLB Most Valuable Player award">Most Valuable Player</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Gold_Glove_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="Gold Glove Award">Gold Glove award</a>. Murphy also won an MVP award the following season, but the Braves began a period of decline that defined the team throughout the 1980s. Murphy, excelling in defense, hitting, and running, was consistently recognized as one of the league\'s best players, but the Braves averaged only 65 wins per season between 1985 and 1990. Their lowest point came in 1988, when they lost 106 games. The 1986 season saw the return of Bobby Cox as <a href="/wiki/General_manager_(baseball)" title="General manager (baseball)">general manager</a>. Also in 1986, the team stopped using their Indian-themed mascot, <a href="/wiki/Chief_Noc-A-Homa" title="Chief Noc-A-Homa">Chief Noc-A-Homa</a>.\n</p>\n<h4><span id="1991.E2.80.932005:_Division_dominance"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1991\xe2\x80\x932005:_Division_dominance">1991\xe2\x80\x932005: Division dominance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlanta_Braves&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: 1991\xe2\x80\x932005: Division dominance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<h5><span id="1991.E2.80.931994"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1991\xe2\x80\x931994">1991\xe2\x80\x931994</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlanta_Braves&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: 1991\xe2\x80\x931994">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:144px;max-width:144px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:142px;max-width:142px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Tom_Glavine_Pitching_1993.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Tom Glavine" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Tom_Glavine_Pitching_1993.jpg/140px-Tom_Glavine_Pitching_1993.jpg" width="140" height="107" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Tom_Glavine_Pitching_1993.jpg/210px-Tom_Glavine_Pitching_1993.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Tom_Glavine_Pitching_1993.jpg/280px-Tom_Glavine_Pitching_1993.jpg 2x" data-file-width="760" data-file-height="580" /></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:142px;max-width:142px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:JohnSmoltz.jpg" class="image"><img alt="John Smoltz" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/JohnSmoltz.jpg/140px-JohnSmoltz.jpg" width="140" height="209" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f0/JohnSmoltz.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="178" data-file-height="266" /></a></div></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><a href="/wiki/Greg_Maddux" title="Greg Maddux">Greg Maddux</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tom_Glavine" title="Tom Glavine">Tom Glavine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_Smoltz" title="John Smoltz">John Smoltz</a> combined for six <a href="/wiki/Cy_Young_Award" title="Cy Young Award">Cy Young Awards</a> during their time in the Braves pitching rotation</div></div></div>\n<p>Cox returned to the dugout as manager in the middle of the 1990 season, replacing <a href="/wiki/Russ_Nixon" title="Russ Nixon">Russ Nixon</a>. The Braves finished the year with the worst record in baseball, at 65\xe2\x80\x9397. They traded Dale Murphy to the <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Phillies" title="Philadelphia Phillies">Philadelphia Phillies</a> after it was clear he was becoming a less dominant player. Pitching coach <a href="/wiki/Leo_Mazzone" title="Leo Mazzone">Leo Mazzone</a> began developing young pitchers <a href="/wiki/Tom_Glavine" title="Tom Glavine">Tom Glavine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steve_Avery" title="Steve Avery">Steve Avery</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_Smoltz" title="John Smoltz">John Smoltz</a> into future stars. That same year, the Braves used the number one overall pick in the <a href="/wiki/1990_Major_League_Baseball_draft" title="1990 Major League Baseball draft">1990 MLB draft</a> to select <a href="/wiki/Chipper_Jones" title="Chipper Jones">Chipper Jones</a>, who became one of the best hitters in team history. Perhaps the Braves\' most important move was not on the field, but in the front office. Immediately after the season, <a href="/wiki/John_Schuerholz" title="John Schuerholz">John Schuerholz</a> was hired away from the <a href="/wiki/Kansas_City_Royals" title="Kansas City Royals">Kansas City Royals</a> as general manager.\n</p><p>The following season, Glavine, Avery, and Smoltz would be recognized as the best young pitchers in the league, winning 52 games among them. Meanwhile, behind position players <a href="/wiki/David_Justice" title="David Justice">David Justice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ron_Gant" title="Ron Gant">Ron Gant</a> and unexpected league Most Valuable Player and batting champion <a href="/wiki/Terry_Pendleton" title="Terry Pendleton">Terry Pendleton</a>, the <a href="/wiki/1991_Atlanta_Braves_season" title="1991 Atlanta Braves season">Braves</a> overcame a 39\xe2\x80\x9340 start, winning 55 of their final 83 games over the last three months of the season and edging the <a href="/wiki/1991_Los_Angeles_Dodgers_season" title="1991 Los Angeles Dodgers season">Los Angeles Dodgers</a> by one game in one of baseball\'s more memorable playoff races. The "Worst to First" Braves, who had not won a divisional title since 1982, captivated the city of Atlanta (and the entire southeast) during their improbable run to the flag. They defeated the <a href="/wiki/1991_Pittsburgh_Pirates_season" title="1991 Pittsburgh Pirates season">Pittsburgh Pirates</a> in a very tightly contested seven-game <a href="/wiki/1991_National_League_Championship_Series" title="1991 National League Championship Series">NLCS</a> only to lose the <a href="/wiki/1991_World_Series" title="1991 World Series">World Series</a>, also in seven games, to the <a href="/wiki/1991_Minnesota_Twins_season" title="1991 Minnesota Twins season">Minnesota Twins</a>. The series, considered by many to be one of the greatest ever, was the first time a team that had finished last in its division one year went to the World Series the next; both the Twins and Braves accomplished the feat.\n</p><p>Despite the 1991 World Series loss, the Braves\' success would continue. In 1992, the <a href="/wiki/1992_Atlanta_Braves_season" title="1992 Atlanta Braves season">Braves</a> returned to the <a href="/wiki/1992_National_League_Championship_Series" title="1992 National League Championship Series">NLCS</a> and once again defeated the <a href="/wiki/1992_Pittsburgh_Pirates_season" title="1992 Pittsburgh Pirates season">Pirates</a> in seven games, culminating in a <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Cabrera_game" class="mw-redirect" title="Francisco Cabrera game">dramatic game seven win</a>. <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Cabrera_(baseball)" title="Francisco Cabrera (baseball)">Francisco Cabrera</a>\'s two-out single that scored <a href="/wiki/David_Justice" title="David Justice">David Justice</a> and Sid Bream capped a three-run rally in the bottom of the ninth inning that gave the Braves a 3\xe2\x80\x932 victory. It was the first time in post season history that the tying and winning runs had scored on a single play in the ninth inning. The Braves lost the <a href="/wiki/1992_World_Series" title="1992 World Series">World Series</a> to the <a href="/wiki/1992_Toronto_Blue_Jays_season" title="1992 Toronto Blue Jays season">Toronto Blue Jays</a>, however. In 1993, the Braves signed <a href="/wiki/Cy_Young_Award" title="Cy Young Award">Cy Young Award</a> winning pitcher <a href="/wiki/Greg_Maddux" title="Greg Maddux">Greg Maddux</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Cubs" title="Chicago Cubs">Chicago Cubs</a>, leading many baseball insiders to declare the team\'s pitching staff the best in baseball. The <a href="/wiki/1993_Atlanta_Braves_season" title="1993 Atlanta Braves season">1993 team</a> posted a franchise-best 104 wins after a dramatic pennant race with the <a href="/wiki/1993_San_Francisco_Giants_season" title="1993 San Francisco Giants season">San Francisco Giants</a>, who won 103 games. The Braves needed a stunning 55\xe2\x80\x9319 finish to edge out the Giants, who led the Braves by nine games in the standings as late as August 11. However, the Braves fell in the <a href="/wiki/1993_National_League_Championship_Series" title="1993 National League Championship Series">NLCS</a> to the <a href="/wiki/1993_Philadelphia_Phillies_season" title="1993 Philadelphia Phillies season">Philadelphia Phillies</a> in six games.\n</p><p>In 1994, in a realignment of the <a href="/wiki/National_League" title="National League">National League</a>\'s divisions following the 1993 expansion, the Braves moved to the Eastern Division.<sup id="cite_ref-Realignment_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Realignment-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> This realignment was the main cause of the team\'s <a href="/wiki/Braves%E2%80%93Mets_rivalry" title="Braves\xe2\x80\x93Mets rivalry">heated rivalry</a> with the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Mets" title="New York Mets">New York Mets</a> during the mid-to-late 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-RivalryWithMets_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RivalryWithMets-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SubwaySeries_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SubwaySeries-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/1994%E2%80%9395_Major_League_Baseball_strike" title="1994\xe2\x80\x9395 Major League Baseball strike">player\'s strike</a> cut short the 1994 season, prior to the division championships, with the Braves six games behind the <a href="/wiki/1994_Montreal_Expos_season" title="1994 Montreal Expos season">Montreal Expos</a> with 48 games left to play.\n</p>\n<h5><span id="1995.E2.80.932005"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1995\xe2\x80\x932005">1995\xe2\x80\x932005</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlanta_Braves&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: 1995\xe2\x80\x932005">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<p>The Braves returned strong the following strike-shortened (144 games instead of the customary 162) year and beat the <a href="/wiki/1995_Cleveland_Indians_season" title="1995 Cleveland Indians season">Cleveland Indians</a> in the <a href="/wiki/1995_World_Series" title="1995 World Series">1995 World Series</a>. This squelched claims by many Braves critics that they were the "<a href="/wiki/Buffalo_Bills" title="Buffalo Bills">Buffalo Bills</a> of Baseball" (January 1996 issue of <i>Beckett <a href="/wiki/Baseball_Card" class="mw-redirect" title="Baseball Card">Baseball Card</a> Monthly</i>). With this World Series victory, the Braves became the first team in Major League Baseball to win world championships in three different cities. With their strong pitching as a constant, the Braves appeared in the <a href="/wiki/1996_World_Series" title="1996 World Series">1996</a> and <a href="/wiki/1999_World_Series" title="1999 World Series">1999 World Series</a> (losing both to the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Yankees" title="New York Yankees">New York Yankees</a>, managed by <a href="/wiki/Joe_Torre" title="Joe Torre">Joe Torre</a>, a former Braves manager),<sup id="cite_ref-SubwaySeries_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SubwaySeries-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> and had a streak of division titles from 1991 to 2005 (three in the Western Division and eleven in the Eastern) interrupted only in 1994 when the strike ended the season early. Pitching was not the only constant in the Braves organization&#160;\xe2\x80\x94Cox was the Braves\' manager, while Schuerholz remained the team\'s GM until after the 2007 season when he was promoted to team president. Terry Pendleton finished his playing career elsewhere, but returned to the Braves system as the hitting coach.\n</p><p>In October 1996, Time Warner acquired Ted Turner\'s Turner Broadcasting System and all of its assets, including its cable channels and the Atlanta Braves. Over the next few years, Ted Turner\'s presence as owner of the team would diminish.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1995_World_Series_trophy.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/1995_World_Series_trophy.JPG/170px-1995_World_Series_trophy.JPG" width="170" height="289" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/1995_World_Series_trophy.JPG/255px-1995_World_Series_trophy.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/1995_World_Series_trophy.JPG/340px-1995_World_Series_trophy.JPG 2x" data-file-width="538" data-file-height="916" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1995_World_Series_trophy.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/1995_World_Series" title="1995 World Series">1995 World Series</a> <a href="/wiki/Commissioner%27s_Trophy_(MLB)" title="Commissioner&#39;s Trophy (MLB)">Commissioner\'s Trophy</a> on display in the <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Allen_Jr._Braves_Museum_and_Hall_of_Fame" title="Ivan Allen Jr. Braves Museum and Hall of Fame">Ivan Allen Jr. Braves Museum and Hall of Fame</a> at <a href="/wiki/Turner_Field" title="Turner Field">Turner Field</a></div></div></div>\n<p>A 95\xe2\x80\x9367 record in <a href="/wiki/2000_World_Series" title="2000 World Series">2000</a> produced a ninth consecutive division title. However, a sweep by the <a href="/wiki/2000_St._Louis_Cardinals_season" title="2000 St. Louis Cardinals season">St. Louis Cardinals</a> in the <a href="/wiki/2000_National_League_Division_Series" title="2000 National League Division Series">National League Division Series</a> prevented the Braves from reaching the <a href="/wiki/2000_National_League_Championship_Series" title="2000 National League Championship Series">NL Championship Series</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-SubwaySeries_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SubwaySeries-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2001, Atlanta won the National League East division yet again, swept the <a href="/wiki/Houston_Astros" title="Houston Astros">Houston Astros</a> in the NLDS, then lost to the <a href="/wiki/Arizona_Diamondbacks" title="Arizona Diamondbacks">Arizona Diamondbacks</a> in the <a href="/wiki/2001_National_League_Championship_Series" title="2001 National League Championship Series">National League Championship Series</a> four games to one. One memorable game the Braves played that year came on September 21, when they played <a href="/wiki/Braves%E2%80%93Mets_rivalry" title="Braves\xe2\x80\x93Mets rivalry">rival</a> <a href="/wiki/New_York_Mets" title="New York Mets">New York Mets</a> in the first major professional sporting event held in New York City since <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">9/11</a>.\n</p><p>In 2002, 2003 and 2004, the Braves won the Eastern division again, but lost in the NLDS in all three years in the same fashion: 3 games to 2 to the <a href="/wiki/2002_San_Francisco_Giants_season" title="2002 San Francisco Giants season">San Francisco Giants</a>, <a href="/wiki/2005_Chicago_Cubs_season" title="2005 Chicago Cubs season">Chicago Cubs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/2006_Houston_Astros_season" title="2006 Houston Astros season">Houston Astros</a>.\n</p>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Cy_Young_dominance">Cy Young dominance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlanta_Braves&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Cy Young dominance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<p>Six National League <a href="/wiki/Cy_Young_Award" title="Cy Young Award">Cy Young Awards</a> in the 1990s were awarded to three Braves pitchers:\n</p>\n<ul><li>In 1991, left-handed pitcher <a href="/wiki/Tom_Glavine" title="Tom Glavine">Tom Glavine</a> received his first award.</li>\n<li>Right-handed pitcher <a href="/wiki/Greg_Maddux" title="Greg Maddux">Greg Maddux</a> won four in a row, three of them with the Braves from 1993 through 1995. His first award came in 1992 with the Cubs.</li>\n<li>In 1996, right-handed pitcher <a href="/wiki/John_Smoltz" title="John Smoltz">John Smoltz</a> received his only Cy Young Award.</li>\n<li>In 1998, Glavine won his second.</li></ul>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="2005:_A_new_generation">2005: A new generation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlanta_Braves&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: 2005: A new generation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/2005_Atlanta_Braves_season" title="2005 Atlanta Braves season">2005 Atlanta Braves season</a></div>\n<p>In 2005, the Braves won the Division championship for the 14th consecutive time from 1991 to 2005. 14 consecutive division titles stands as the record for all major league baseball. The 2005 title marked the first time any MLB team made the postseason with more than 4 rookies who each had more than 100 ABs (<a href="/wiki/Wilson_Betemit" title="Wilson Betemit">Wilson Betemit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brian_McCann_(baseball)" title="Brian McCann (baseball)">Brian McCann</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pete_Orr" title="Pete Orr">Pete Orr</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ryan_Langerhans" title="Ryan Langerhans">Ryan Langerhans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jeff_Francoeur" title="Jeff Francoeur">Jeff Francoeur</a>).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2007)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Catcher Brian McCann, right fielder Jeff Francoeur, and pitcher <a href="/wiki/Kyle_Davies_(baseball)" title="Kyle Davies (baseball)">Kyle Davies</a> all grew up in the suburbs of Atlanta. The large number of rookies to debut in 2005 were nicknamed the "Baby Braves" by fans and became an Atlanta-area sensation, helping to lead the club to a record of 90\xe2\x80\x9372.\n</p><p>However, the season would end on a sour note as the Braves lost the <a href="/wiki/2005_National_League_Division_Series" title="2005 National League Division Series">National League Division Series</a> to the <a href="/wiki/2005_Houston_Astros_season" title="2005 Houston Astros season">Astros</a> in four games. In Game 4, with the Braves leading by 5 in the eighth inning, the Astros battled back with a <a href="/wiki/Lance_Berkman" title="Lance Berkman">Lance Berkman</a> <a href="/wiki/Grand_slam_(baseball)" title="Grand slam (baseball)">grand slam</a> and a two-out, ninth inning <a href="/wiki/Brad_Ausmus" title="Brad Ausmus">Brad Ausmus</a> home run off of Braves closer <a href="/wiki/Kyle_Farnsworth" title="Kyle Farnsworth">Kyle Farnsworth</a>. The game did not end until the 18th inning, becoming the longest game in playoff history at 5 hours 50&#160;minutes. <a href="/wiki/Chris_Burke_(baseball)" title="Chris Burke (baseball)">Chris Burke</a> ended the marathon with a home run off of <a href="/wiki/Joey_Devine" title="Joey Devine">Joey Devine</a>.\n</p><p>After the 2005 season, the Braves lost their long-time pitching coach <a href="/wiki/Leo_Mazzone" title="Leo Mazzone">Leo Mazzone</a>, who left to go to the <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_Orioles" title="Baltimore Orioles">Baltimore Orioles</a>. <a href="/wiki/Roger_McDowell" title="Roger McDowell">Roger McDowell</a> took his place in the Atlanta dugout. Unable to re-sign shortstop <a href="/wiki/Rafael_Furcal" title="Rafael Furcal">Rafael Furcal</a>, the Braves acquired shortstop <a href="/wiki/%C3%89dgar_Renter%C3%ADa" title="\xc3\x89dgar Renter\xc3\xada">\xc3\x89dgar Renter\xc3\xada</a> from the Boston Red Sox.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2006:_Struggles">2006: Struggles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlanta_Braves&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: 2006: Struggles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/2006_Atlanta_Braves_season" title="2006 Atlanta Braves season">2006 Atlanta Braves season</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Andruw_Jones.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Andruw_Jones.jpg/220px-Andruw_Jones.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Andruw_Jones.jpg/330px-Andruw_Jones.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Andruw_Jones.jpg/440px-Andruw_Jones.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="533" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Andruw_Jones.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Andruw_Jones" title="Andruw Jones">Andruw Jones</a> at bat for the Braves in 2006</div></div></div>\n<p>In 2006, the Braves did not perform at the level they had grown accustomed to. Due to an offensive slump, injuries to their starting rotation, and subpar bullpen performances, the Braves compiled a 6\xe2\x80\x9321 record during June, the worst month ever in Atlanta with a winning percentage of .222; this was only better than the woeful <a href="/wiki/1935_Boston_Braves_season" title="1935 Boston Braves season">Boston Braves</a> in May 1935 (4\xe2\x80\x9320) with a .166 winning percentage.\n</p><p>After the break, the Braves came out with their bats swinging, setting many franchise records. They won five straight, sweeping the Padres and taking two from the Cardinals, tallying a total of 65 runs in that span. The 65 runs in five games is the best by the franchise since 1897, when the <a href="/wiki/1897_Boston_Beaneaters_season" title="1897 Boston Beaneaters season">Boston Beaneaters</a> totaled 78, including 25 in one game and 21 in another, from May 31 \xe2\x80\x93 June 3; the 2006 Braves also became the first team since the <a href="/wiki/1930_New_York_Yankees_season" title="1930 New York Yankees season">1930 New York Yankees</a> to score ten runs or more in five straight games. The Braves had a total of 81 hits during their five-game run and 98 hits in their last six games, going back to an 8\xe2\x80\x933 victory over <a href="/wiki/2006_Cincinnati_Reds_season" title="2006 Cincinnati Reds season">Cincinnati</a> on July 9, the last game before the All-Star break. Additionally, Chipper Jones was able to maintain a 20-game hitting streak and tie <a href="/wiki/Paul_Waner" title="Paul Waner">Paul Waner</a>\'s 69-year-old Major League record with a 14-game extra-base hit streak.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, on September 18, the New York Mets\' win over the <a href="/wiki/2006_Florida_Marlins_season" title="2006 Florida Marlins season">Florida Marlins</a> mathematically eliminated the Braves from winning the NL East, ending the Atlanta Braves\' 11-year reign over the NL East. On September 24, the Braves\' loss to the <a href="/wiki/2006_Colorado_Rockies_season" title="2006 Colorado Rockies season">Colorado Rockies</a> mathematically eliminated the Braves from winning the NL Wild Card, making 2006 the first year that the Braves would not compete in the postseason since 1990, not counting the strike-shortened 1994 season. Also, a loss to the Mets on September 28 guaranteed the Braves their first losing season since 1990. Although the Braves won two of their last three games against the Astros, including rookie <a href="/wiki/Chuck_James" title="Chuck James">Chuck James</a> besting <a href="/wiki/Roger_Clemens" title="Roger Clemens">Roger Clemens</a>, Atlanta finished the season in third place, one game ahead of the Marlins, at 79\xe2\x80\x9383.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sale_to_Liberty_Media">Sale to Liberty Media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlanta_Braves&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Sale to Liberty Media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In December 2005, team owner <a href="/wiki/Time_Warner" class="mw-redirect" title="Time Warner">Time Warner</a>, who inherited the Braves after purchasing <a href="/wiki/Turner_Broadcasting_System" title="Turner Broadcasting System">TBS</a> in 1996, announced it was placing the team for sale.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Liberty_Media" title="Liberty Media">Liberty Media</a> began negotiations to purchase the team.\n</p><p>In February 2007, after more than a year of negotiations, Time Warner agreed to a deal that would sell the Braves to <a href="/wiki/Liberty_Media" title="Liberty Media">Liberty Media Group</a> (a company which owned a large amount of stock in <a href="/wiki/Time_Warner" class="mw-redirect" title="Time Warner">Time Warner, Inc.</a>), pending approval by 75 percent of MLB owners and the Commissioner of Baseball, <a href="/wiki/Bud_Selig" title="Bud Selig">Bud Selig</a>. The deal included the exchange of the Braves, valued in the deal at $450&#160;million, a hobbyist magazine publishing company, and $980&#160;million cash, for 68.5&#160;million shares of Time Warner stock held by Liberty Media, then worth approximately $1.48&#160;billion. Team President Terry McGuirk anticipated no change in the current front office structure, personnel, or day-to-day operations of the Braves. Liberty Media is not expected to take any type of "active" ownership in terms of day-to-day operations.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On May 16, 2007, Major League Baseball\'s owners approved the sale of the Braves from Time Warner to Liberty Media.<sup id="cite_ref-BravesSaleApproved_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BravesSaleApproved-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The Braves are one of only two Major League Baseball teams under majority corporate ownership (and the only NL team with this distinction);
